   <h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading">Pentecost</h1> 
   <div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content"> 
    <div id="siteSub" class="noprint">
     From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia
    </div> 
    <div id="contentSub"></div> 
    <div id="contentSub2"></div> 
    <div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a> 
    <div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr">
     <div class="mw-parser-output">
      <div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">
       Christian holiday commemorating the Holy Spirit's descent upon the Apostles
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       This article is about the Christian holiday. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Pentecost_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Pentecost (disambiguation)">Pentecost (disambiguation)</a>.
      </div>

      <p>The Christian holiday of <b>Pentecost</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Moveable_feast" title="Moveable feast">moveable feast</a>, which is celebrated on the 50th day (the seventh Sunday) from <a href="/wiki/Easter_Sunday" class="mw-redirect" title="Easter Sunday">Easter Sunday</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Pritchard2019_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pritchard2019-2">[2]</a></sup> It commemorates the descent of the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Spirit" title="Holy Spirit">Holy Spirit</a> upon the <a href="/wiki/Apostle_(Christian)" class="mw-redirect" title="Apostle (Christian)">Apostles</a> and other followers of <a href="/wiki/Jesus_Christ" class="mw-redirect" title="Jesus Christ">Jesus Christ</a> while they were in Jerusalem celebrating the <a href="/wiki/Feast_of_Weeks" class="mw-redirect" title="Feast of Weeks">Feast of Weeks</a>, as described in the <a href="/wiki/Acts_of_the_Apostles" title="Acts of the Apostles">Acts of the Apostles</a> (<a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/Acts#2:1" class="extiw" title="s:Bible (King James)/Acts">Acts 2:1–31</a>. </p>
      <p>The holiday is also called "White Sunday" or "<a href="/wiki/Whitsunday" class="mw-redirect" title="Whitsunday">Whitsunday</a>" or "Whitsun", especially in the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a>, where traditionally the next day, <a href="/wiki/Whit_Monday" title="Whit Monday">Whit Monday</a>, was also a public holiday (since 1971 fixed by statute on the last Monday in May). The Monday after Pentecost is a legal holiday in many European countries. </p>
      <p>In <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Christianity" title="Eastern Christianity">Eastern Christianity</a>, Pentecost can also refer to the entire fifty days of <a href="/wiki/Easter" title="Easter">Easter</a> through Pentecost inclusive; hence the book containing the liturgical texts is called the "<i><a href="/wiki/Pentecostarion" title="Pentecostarion">Pentecostarion</a></i>". Since its date depends on the <a href="/wiki/Date_of_Easter" title="Date of Easter">date of Easter</a>, Pentecost is a "<a href="/wiki/Moveable_feast" title="Moveable feast">moveable feast</a>". </p>
      <p>Pentecost is one of the <a href="/wiki/Great_feasts_in_the_Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Great feasts in the Eastern Orthodox Church">Great feasts in the Eastern Orthodox Church</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Solemnity" title="Solemnity">Solemnity</a> in the Roman Rite of the Catholic Church, a <a href="/wiki/Liturgical_calendar_(Lutheran)#Festivals" title="Liturgical calendar (Lutheran)">Festival</a> in the Lutheran Churches, and a <a href="/wiki/Principal_Feast" title="Principal Feast">Principal Feast</a> in the Anglican Communion. Many Christian denominations provide a special liturgy for this holy celebration. </p>

      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pentecost&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <p>The term Pentecost comes from the Greek <span lang="el" title="Greek-language text">Πεντηκοστή</span> (<i lang="grc-Latn" title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language romanization">Pentēkostē</i>) meaning "fiftieth". It refers to the Jewish festival celebrated on the fiftieth day after <a href="/wiki/Passover" title="Passover">Passover</a>, also known as the "Feast of Weeks"<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[i]</a></sup> and the "Feast of 50 days" in <a href="/wiki/Rabbinic_tradition" class="mw-redirect" title="Rabbinic tradition">rabbinic tradition</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BDAG_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BDAG-6">[5]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Septuagint" title="Septuagint">Septuagint</a> uses the term <i lang="grc-Latn" title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language romanization">Pentēkostē</i> to refer to the "Feast of Pentecost" only twice, in the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Tobit" title="Book of Tobit">Book of Tobit</a> and <a href="/wiki/2_Maccabees" title="2 Maccabees">2 Maccabees</a>, which are <a href="/wiki/Deuterocanonical_books" title="Deuterocanonical books">deuterocanonical</a>/<a href="/wiki/Biblical_apocrypha" title="Biblical apocrypha">apocryphal</a> books in the <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">Bible</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-TDNT_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TDNT-7">[6]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-isbe_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-isbe-8">[7]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[8]</a></sup> The term Pentecost appears in the Septuagint as one of names for the Festival of Weeks.<sup id="cite_ref-jansen_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jansen-10">[9]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The Septuagint writers also used the word in two other senses: to signify the year of <a href="/wiki/Jubilee_(biblical)" title="Jubilee (biblical)">Jubilee</a> (<a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/Leviticus#25:10" class="extiw" title="s:Bible (King James)/Leviticus">Leviticus 25:10</a>),<sup id="cite_ref-TDNT_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TDNT-7">[6]</a></sup> an event which occurs every 50th year, and in several passages of chronology as an <a href="/wiki/Ordinal_number" title="Ordinal number">ordinal number</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[ii]</a></sup> The term has also been used in the literature of <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_Judaism" title="Hellenistic Judaism">Hellenistic Judaism</a> by <a href="/wiki/Philo_of_Alexandria" class="mw-redirect" title="Philo of Alexandria">Philo of Alexandria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Josephus" title="Josephus">Josephus</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BDAG_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BDAG-6">[5]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Background">Background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pentecost&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Shavuot" title="Shavuot">Shavuot</a>
      </div> 
      <p>In <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Judaism</a> the Festival of Weeks (<a href="/wiki/Hebrew_language" title="Hebrew language">Hebrew</a>: <span lang="he" dir="rtl">שבועות</span>‎ <i>Shavuot</i>) is a <a href="/wiki/Harvest_festival" title="Harvest festival">harvest festival</a> that is celebrated seven weeks and one day after the first day of the Feast of Unleavened Bread in <a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/Deuteronomy#16:9" class="extiw" title="s:Bible (King James)/Deuteronomy">Deuteronomy 16:9</a> or seven weeks and one day after the <a href="/wiki/Sabbath" title="Sabbath">Sabbath</a> referred to in <a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/Leviticus#23:16" class="extiw" title="s:Bible (King James)/Leviticus">Leviticus 23:16</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-EDNT_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EDNT-13">[11]</a></sup> The Festival of Weeks is also called the <i>feast of Harvest</i> in <a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/Exodus#23:16" class="extiw" title="s:Bible (King James)/Exodus">Exodus 23:16</a> and the <i>day of first fruits</i> in <a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/Numbers#28:26" class="extiw" title="s:Bible (King James)/Numbers">Numbers 28:26</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-keil_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-keil-14">[12]</a></sup> In <a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/Exodus#34:22" class="extiw" title="s:Bible (King James)/Exodus">Exodus 34:22</a> it is called the "firstfruits of the wheat harvest."<sup id="cite_ref-EBC_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EBC-15">[13]</a></sup> The date for the "Feast of Weeks" originally came the day after seven full weeks following the first harvest of grain.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[14]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[15]</a></sup> In Jewish tradition the fiftieth day was known as the <i>Festival of Weeks</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-keil_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-keil-14">[12]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-EBC_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EBC-15">[13]</a></sup> The actual mention of fifty days comes from <a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/Leviticus#23:16" class="extiw" title="s:Bible (King James)/Leviticus">Leviticus 23:16</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-auto_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-3">[3]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-niv_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-niv-18">[16]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>During the Hellenistic period, the ancient harvest festival also became a day of renewing the <a href="/wiki/Seven_laws_of_Noah" class="mw-redirect" title="Seven laws of Noah">Noahic covenant</a>, described in <a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/Genesis#9:8" class="extiw" title="s:Bible (King James)/Genesis">Genesis 9:8-17</a>, which is established between God and "all flesh that is upon the earth".<sup id="cite_ref-jansen_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jansen-10">[9]</a></sup> By this time, some Jews were already living in <a href="/wiki/Diaspora" title="Diaspora">Diaspora</a>. According to <a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/Acts#2:5" class="extiw" title="s:Bible (King James)/Acts">Acts 2:5-11</a> there were Jews from "every nation under heaven" in Jerusalem, possibly visiting the city as <a href="/wiki/Pilgrims" class="mw-redirect" title="Pilgrims">pilgrims</a> during Pentecost. In particular the <i>hoi epidemountes</i> (<span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text">οἱ ἐπιδημοῦντες</span>) are identified as "visitors" to Jerusalem from Rome. This group of visitors includes both Jews and "proselytes" (<span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text">προσήλυτος</span>, <i>prosēlytos</i>); sometimes translated as "converts to Judaism", <i>proselyte</i> referred to non-Jews who adhered fully to the <a href="/wiki/Mosaic_laws" class="mw-redirect" title="Mosaic laws">Mosaic laws</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Religious_male_circumcision" title="Religious male circumcision">circumcision</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ebcacts_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ebcacts-19">[17]</a></sup> The list of nations represented in the biblical text includes <a href="/wiki/Parthia" title="Parthia">Parthians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Medes" title="Medes">Medes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Elamites" class="mw-redirect" title="Elamites">Elamites</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Judaea" class="mw-redirect" title="Judaea">Judaea</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[iii]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cappadocia" title="Cappadocia">Cappadocia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pontus_(region)" title="Pontus (region)">Pontus</a>, Asia, <a href="/wiki/Phrygia" title="Phrygia">Phrygia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pamphylia" title="Pamphylia">Pamphylia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cyrene,_Libya" title="Cyrene, Libya">Cyrene</a>, and those who were visiting from <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Rome</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-arch_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-arch-21">[18]</a></sup> Scholars have speculated about a possible earlier literary source for the list of nations including an astrological list by <a href="/wiki/Paul_of_Alexandria" class="mw-redirect" title="Paul of Alexandria">Paul of Alexandria</a> and various references to the Jewish diaspora by writers of the <a href="/wiki/Second_Temple" title="Second Temple">Second Temple</a> era (particularly <a href="/wiki/Philo_of_Alexandria" class="mw-redirect" title="Philo of Alexandria">Philo of Alexandria</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-gilbert_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gilbert-22">[19]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>After the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Jerusalem_(70_CE)" title="Siege of Jerusalem (70 CE)">destruction of the temple</a> in 70 AD offerings could no longer be brought to the Temple and the focus of the festival shifted from <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">agriculture</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Law_given_to_Moses_at_Sinai" title="Law given to Moses at Sinai">giving of the law on Sinai</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-EDNT_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EDNT-13">[11]</a></sup> It became customary to gather at <a href="/wiki/Synagogue" title="Synagogue">synagogue</a> and read the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Ruth" title="Book of Ruth">Book of Ruth</a> and <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Exodus" title="Book of Exodus">Exodus</a> Chapters 19 and 20.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="New_Testament">New Testament</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pentecost&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: New Testament">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Cenacle_on_Mount_Zion.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Cenacle_on_Mount_Zion.jpg/220px-Cenacle_on_Mount_Zion.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Cenacle_on_Mount_Zion.jpg/330px-Cenacle_on_Mount_Zion.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Cenacle_on_Mount_Zion.jpg/440px-Cenacle_on_Mount_Zion.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="600"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Cenacle_on_Mount_Zion.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The <a href="/wiki/Cenacle" title="Cenacle">Cenacle</a> in <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem" title="Jerusalem">Jerusalem</a> is claimed to be the location of the <a href="/wiki/Last_Supper" title="Last Supper">Last Supper</a> and Pentecost.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[20]</a></sup>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The narrative in <a href="/wiki/Acts_2" title="Acts 2">Acts 2</a> of the Pentecost includes numerous references to earlier biblical narratives like the <a href="/wiki/Tower_of_Babel" title="Tower of Babel">Tower of Babel</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Genesis_flood_narrative" title="Genesis flood narrative">flood</a> and <a href="/wiki/Genesis_creation_narrative" title="Genesis creation narrative">creation</a> narratives from the Book of Genesis. It also includes references to certain <a href="/wiki/Theophany" title="Theophany">theophanies</a>, with certain emphasis on God's incarnate appearance on <a href="/wiki/Biblical_Mount_Sinai" class="mw-redirect" title="Biblical Mount Sinai">biblical Mount Sinai</a> when the <a href="/wiki/Ten_Commandments" title="Ten Commandments">Ten Commandments</a> were presented to <a href="/wiki/Moses" title="Moses">Moses</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-jansen_10-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jansen-10">[9]</a></sup> Theologian Stephen Wilson has described the narrative as "exceptionally obscure" and various points of disagreement persist among bible scholars.<sup id="cite_ref-gilbert_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gilbert-22">[19]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Some biblical commentators have sought to establish that the <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text">οἶκος</span> (house) given as the location of the events of in Acts 2:2 was one of the thirty halls of the Temple (called <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text">οίκοι</span>), but the text itself is lacking in specific details. <a href="/wiki/Richard_C._H._Lenski" title="Richard C. H. Lenski">Richard C. H. Lenski</a> and other scholars contend that the author of Acts could have chosen the word <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text">ἱερόν</span> (sanctuary or temple) if this meaning were intended, rather than "house".<sup id="cite_ref-ebcacts_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ebcacts-19">[17]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Lenski_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lenski-24">[21]</a></sup> Some semantic details suggest that the "house" could be the "upper room" (<span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text">ὑπερῷον</span>) mentioned in <a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/Acts#1:12" class="extiw" title="s:Bible (King James)/Acts">Acts 1:12-26</a>, but there is no literary evidence to confirm the location with certainty and it remains a subject of dispute amongst scholars.<sup id="cite_ref-jansen_10-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jansen-10">[9]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ebcacts_19-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ebcacts-19">[17]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Acts">Acts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pentecost&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Acts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Schnorr_von_Carolsfeld_Bibel_in_Bildern_1860_226.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Schnorr_von_Carolsfeld_Bibel_in_Bildern_1860_226.png/220px-Schnorr_von_Carolsfeld_Bibel_in_Bildern_1860_226.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="178" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Schnorr_von_Carolsfeld_Bibel_in_Bildern_1860_226.png/330px-Schnorr_von_Carolsfeld_Bibel_in_Bildern_1860_226.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Schnorr_von_Carolsfeld_Bibel_in_Bildern_1860_226.png/440px-Schnorr_von_Carolsfeld_Bibel_in_Bildern_1860_226.png 2x" data-file-width="1107" data-file-height="896"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Schnorr_von_Carolsfeld_Bibel_in_Bildern_1860_226.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Pentecost by <a href="/wiki/Julius_Schnorr_von_Carolsfeld" title="Julius Schnorr von Carolsfeld">Julius Schnorr von Carolsfeld</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:1472_map_of_Jerusalem_by_Hugo_Comminelli_and_Pietro_del_Massaio_01.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/1472_map_of_Jerusalem_by_Hugo_Comminelli_and_Pietro_del_Massaio_01.jpg/220px-1472_map_of_Jerusalem_by_Hugo_Comminelli_and_Pietro_del_Massaio_01.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="307" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/1472_map_of_Jerusalem_by_Hugo_Comminelli_and_Pietro_del_Massaio_01.jpg/330px-1472_map_of_Jerusalem_by_Hugo_Comminelli_and_Pietro_del_Massaio_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/1472_map_of_Jerusalem_by_Hugo_Comminelli_and_Pietro_del_Massaio_01.jpg/440px-1472_map_of_Jerusalem_by_Hugo_Comminelli_and_Pietro_del_Massaio_01.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="1429"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:1472_map_of_Jerusalem_by_Hugo_Comminelli_and_Pietro_del_Massaio_01.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>This 1472 <a href="/wiki/Cartography_of_Jerusalem" title="Cartography of Jerusalem">map of Jerusalem</a> notes the place of the Pentecost, "Ubi apostoli acceperunt spiritum sanctum", at the location of the <a href="/wiki/Cenacle" title="Cenacle">Cenacle</a>.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The events of Acts Chapter 2 are set against the backdrop of the celebration of Pentecost in Jerusalem. There are several major features to the Pentecost narrative presented in the second chapter of the <a href="/wiki/Acts_of_the_Apostles" title="Acts of the Apostles">Acts of the Apostles</a>. The author begins by noting that the disciples of Jesus "were all together in one place" on the "day of Pentecost" (<span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text">ημέρα της Πεντηκοστής</span>).<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[22]</a></sup> The verb used in Acts 2:1 to indicate the arrival of the day of Pentecost carries a connotation of fulfillment.<sup id="cite_ref-Lenski_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lenski-24">[21]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-vine_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vine-26">[23]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Calvin_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Calvin-27">[24]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>There is a "mighty rushing wind" (wind is a common symbol for the Holy Spirit)<sup id="cite_ref-Calvin_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Calvin-27">[24]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[25]</a></sup> and "tongues as of fire" appear.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[26]</a></sup> The gathered disciples were "filled with the Holy Spirit, and began to speak in other tongues as the Spirit gave them utterance".<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[27]</a></sup> Some scholars have interpreted the passage as a reference to the multitude of languages spoken by the gathered disciples,<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[28]</a></sup> while others have taken the reference to "tongues" (<span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text">γλώσσαι</span>) to signify <a href="/wiki/Ecstatic" class="mw-redirect" title="Ecstatic">ecstatic</a> speech.<sup id="cite_ref-gilbert_22-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gilbert-22">[19]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[29]</a></sup> In Christian tradition, this event represents fulfillment of the promise that Christ will <a href="/wiki/Baptize" class="mw-redirect" title="Baptize">baptize</a> his followers with the Holy Spirit.<sup id="cite_ref-Lenski_24-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lenski-24">[21]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[30]</a></sup> (Out of the four New Testament gospels, the distinction between baptism by water and the baptism by Christ with "Holy Spirit and fire" is only found in <a href="/wiki/The_Book_of_Matthew" class="mw-redirect" title="The Book of Matthew">Matthew</a> and Luke.)<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[31]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[32]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The narrative in Acts evokes the symbolism of Jesus' baptism in the <a href="/wiki/Jordan_River" title="Jordan River">Jordan River</a>, and the start of his ministry, by explicitly connecting the earlier prophecy of <a href="/wiki/John_the_Baptist" title="John the Baptist">John the Baptist</a> to the baptism of the disciples with the Holy Spirit on the day of Pentecost.<sup id="cite_ref-ebcacts_19-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ebcacts-19">[17]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[33]</a></sup> The timing of the narrative during the law giving festival of Pentecost symbolizes both continuity with the giving of the law, but also the central role of the Holy Spirit (understood as an aspect of Jesus Christ) for the early Church. The central role of Christ in Christian faith signified a fundamental theological separation from the traditional Jewish faith, which was grounded in the Torah and Mosaic Law.<sup id="cite_ref-ebcacts_19-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ebcacts-19">[17]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Peter's sermon in <a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/Acts#2:14" class="extiw" title="s:Bible (King James)/Acts">Acts 2:14–36</a> stresses the <a href="/wiki/Resurrection_of_Jesus" title="Resurrection of Jesus">resurrection</a> and <a href="/wiki/Session_of_Christ" title="Session of Christ">exaltation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-isbe_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-isbe-8">[7]</a></sup> In his sermon, Peter quotes <a href="/wiki/Joel_2:28%E2%80%9332" class="mw-redirect" title="Joel 2:28–32">Joel 2:28–32</a> and <a href="/wiki/Psalm_16" title="Psalm 16">Psalm 16</a> to indicate that first Pentecost marks the start of the <a href="/wiki/Messianic_Age" title="Messianic Age">Messianic Age</a>. About one hundred and twenty followers of Christ (Acts 1:15) were present, including the Twelve Apostles (<a href="/wiki/Saint_Matthias" title="Saint Matthias">Matthias</a> was <a href="/wiki/Judas" class="mw-redirect" title="Judas">Judas</a>' replacement) (Acts 1:13, 26), Jesus' mother Mary, other female disciples and his brothers (Acts 1:14).<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[34]</a></sup> While those on whom the Spirit had descended were speaking in many languages, the <a href="/wiki/Saint_Peter" title="Saint Peter">Apostle Peter</a> stood up with the <a href="/wiki/Apostle_(Christian)" class="mw-redirect" title="Apostle (Christian)">eleven</a> and proclaimed to the crowd that this event was the fulfillment of the prophecy.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[35]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Acts_2#Verses_16–21" title="Acts 2">Acts 2:17</a>, it reads: "'And in the last days,' God says, 'I will pour out my spirit upon every sort of flesh, and your sons and your daughters will prophesy and your young men will see visions and your old men will dream dreams." He also mentions (<a href="/wiki/Acts_2:15" class="mw-redirect" title="Acts 2:15">Acts 2:15</a>) that it was the third hour of the day (about 9:00&nbsp;am). <a href="/wiki/Acts_2:41" class="mw-redirect" title="Acts 2:41">Acts 2:41</a> then reports: "Then they that gladly received his word were baptized: and the same day there were added unto them about three thousand souls."<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[36]</a></sup> Critical scholars believe some features of the narrative are theological constructions. Scholars believe that even if the Pentecost narrative is not literally true, it does signify an important event in the history of the early Church which enabled the rapid spread of Christianity. Within a few decades important congregations had been established in all major cities of the Roman Empire.<sup id="cite_ref-isbe_8-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-isbe-8">[7]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Biblical commentator <a href="/wiki/Richard_C._H._Lenski" title="Richard C. H. Lenski">Richard C. H. Lenski</a> has noted that the use of the term "Pentecost" in Acts is a reference to the Jewish festival. He writes that a well-defined, distinct Christian celebration did not exist until later years, when Christians kept the name of "Pentecost" but began to calculate the date of the feast based on Easter rather than Passover.<sup id="cite_ref-Lenski_24-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lenski-24">[21]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Peter stated that this event was the beginning of a continual outpouring that would be available to all believers from that point on, Jews and Gentiles alike.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[37]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span id="Leo.27s_sermons"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Leo's_sermons">Leo's sermons</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pentecost&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Leo's sermons">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>Scholars believe that <a href="/wiki/Pope_Leo_I" title="Pope Leo I">Pope Leo I</a>'s <i>Sermons 75–77</i> were given on Pentecost Sunday. In these sermons, dating to the 5th century, Leo discusses Macedonianism, which the Catholic Church denounced as heresy because Macedonianism considered the Holy Spirit inferior to the Father and Son, thereby undermining the <a href="/wiki/Trinity" title="Trinity">Trinity</a>. He also denounces <a href="/wiki/Manichean" class="mw-redirect" title="Manichean">Manichean</a> doctrine on the Holy Spirit, which taught that the Holy Spirit was present in <a href="/wiki/Mani_(prophet)" title="Mani (prophet)">Mani</a>. Leo discusses other Catholic doctrine pertaining to the Holy Spirit, especially emphasizing the Spirit's relationship to the Father and Son of the Trinity and the Catholic Church. He draws an analogy between Jewish practices and the Christian feast day: "As once to the Hebrew people, freed from Egypt, the law was given on Mt. Sinai on the fiftieth day after the sacrifice of the lamb, so after the Passion of the Christ when the true Lamb of God was killed, on the fiftieth day from his Resurrection, the Holy Spirit came down on the apostles and the community of believers."<sup id="cite_ref-Catholic_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Catholic-41">[38]</a></sup> Leo calls this the <a href="/wiki/Second_Covenant" class="mw-redirect" title="Second Covenant">Second Covenant</a> and says that it is "established by the same Spirit who has set up the first". He describes the descent of the Holy Spirit upon the disciples on Pentecost as the fulfillment of a "long-awaited promise".<sup id="cite_ref-Catholic_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Catholic-41">[38]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span id="Apocryphal.2FDeuterocanonical_books"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Apocryphal/Deuterocanonical_books">Apocryphal/Deuterocanonical books</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pentecost&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Apocryphal/Deuterocanonical books">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <p>In <a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/Tobit#2:1" class="extiw" title="s:Bible (King James)/Tobit">Tobit 2:1</a> <i lang="grc-Latn" title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language romanization">Pentēkostē</i> is used as an alternate name for the Jewish holiday of <i>Shavuot</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-ubstobit&amp;judith_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ubstobit&amp;judith-42">[39]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/NABRE" class="mw-redirect" title="NABRE">NABRE</a> translation of this passage reads: "on our festival of Pentecost, the holy feast of Weeks".<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[40]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Liturgical_celebration">Liturgical celebration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pentecost&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Liturgical celebration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <table class="box-More_citations_needed plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation">
       <tbody>
        <tr>
         <td class="mbox-image">
          <div style="width:52px">

          </div></td>

          <div class="mbox-text-span">
