      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Eastern_churches"><span class="anchor" id="Kneeling_Prayer"></span> Eastern churches</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pentecost&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Eastern churches">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>In the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">Eastern Orthodox Church</a>, Pentecost is one of the Orthodox <a href="/wiki/Great_Feasts" class="mw-redirect" title="Great Feasts">Great Feasts</a> and is considered to be the highest ranking Great Feast of the Lord, second in rank only to <a href="/wiki/Easter" title="Easter">Easter</a>. The service is celebrated with an <a href="/wiki/All-night_Vigil" class="mw-redirect" title="All-night Vigil">All-night Vigil</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Eve" title="Eve">eve</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Feast_day" class="mw-redirect" title="Feast day">feast day</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Divine_Liturgy" title="Divine Liturgy">Divine Liturgy</a> on the day of the feast itself. <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_church_architecture" title="Eastern Orthodox church architecture">Orthodox churches</a> are often decorated with greenery and flowers on this feast day, and the celebration is intentionally similar to the Jewish holiday of <a href="/wiki/Shavuot" title="Shavuot">Shavuot</a>, which celebrates the giving of the <a href="/wiki/Mosaic_Law" class="mw-redirect" title="Mosaic Law">Mosaic Law</a>. </p>
      <p>The feast itself lasts three days. The first day is known as "<a href="/wiki/Trinity_Sunday" title="Trinity Sunday">Trinity Sunday</a>"; the second day is known as "<a href="/wiki/Spirit_Monday" class="mw-redirect" title="Spirit Monday">Spirit Monday</a>" (or "Monday of the Holy Spirit"); and the third day, Tuesday, is called the "<a href="/wiki/Third_Day_of_the_Trinity" class="mw-redirect" title="Third Day of the Trinity">Third Day of the Trinity</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[41]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Afterfeast" title="Afterfeast">Afterfeast</a> of Pentecost lasts for one week, during which <a href="/wiki/Fasting" title="Fasting">fasting</a> is not permitted, even on Wednesday and Friday. In the Orthodox <a href="/wiki/Sacred_Tradition" class="mw-redirect" title="Sacred Tradition">Tradition</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Liturgical_color" class="mw-redirect" title="Liturgical color">liturgical color</a> used at Pentecost is <a href="/wiki/Green" title="Green">green</a>, and the clergy and faithful carry flowers and green branches in their hands during the services. </p>
      <p>A popular tradition arose in both west and east of decorating the church with roses on Pentecost, leading to a popular designation of Pentecost as <a href="/wiki/Latin_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Latin language">Latin</a>: <i lang="la">Festa Rosalia</i> or "Rose Feast"; in Greek this became <span lang="el" title="Greek-language text">ρουσάλια</span> (<i lang="el-Latn" title="Greek-language romanization">rousália</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-byzcatharchpittspent_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-byzcatharchpittspent-45">[42]</a></sup> This led to <i>Rusalii</i> becoming the <a href="/wiki/Romanian_language" title="Romanian language">Romanian language</a> term for the feast, as well as the <a href="/wiki/Neapolitan_language" title="Neapolitan language">Neapolitan</a> popular designation <i>Pasca rusata</i> ("rosey Easter").<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> In modern times, the term in Greek refers to the eve of Pentecost, not Pentecost itself; or, in the case of <a href="/wiki/Megara" title="Megara">Megara</a> in <a href="/wiki/Attica" title="Attica">Attica</a>, to the Monday and Tuesday after <i>Pascha</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-endemicgreekrousalia_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-endemicgreekrousalia-46">[43]</a></sup> as roses are often used during the whole liturgical season of the <a href="/wiki/Pentecostarion" title="Pentecostarion">Pentecostarion</a>, not just Pentecost. <a href="/wiki/John_Chrysostom" title="John Chrysostom">John Chrysostom</a> warned his flock not to allow this custom to replace spiritually adorning themselves with virtue in reception of the <a href="/wiki/Fruits_of_the_Holy_Spirit" class="mw-redirect" title="Fruits of the Holy Spirit">Fruits of the Holy Spirit</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-byzcatharchpittspent_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-byzcatharchpittspent-45">[42]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>An extraordinary service called the "Kneeling Prayer" is observed on the night of Pentecost. This is a <a href="/wiki/Great_Vespers" class="mw-redirect" title="Great Vespers">Vespers</a> service to which are added three sets of long poetical prayers, the composition of Saint <a href="/wiki/Basil_the_Great" class="mw-redirect" title="Basil the Great">Basil the Great</a>, during which everyone makes a full <a href="/wiki/Genuflection#Orthodox_Christianity" title="Genuflection">prostration</a>, touching their foreheads to the floor (prostrations in church having been forbidden from the day of <a href="/wiki/Easter" title="Easter">Pascha</a> (Easter) up to this point). Uniquely, these prayers include a petition for all of those in hell, that they may be granted relief and even ultimate release from their confinement, if God deems this possible.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[44]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>All of the remaining days of the <a href="/wiki/Ecclesiastical_year" class="mw-redirect" title="Ecclesiastical year">ecclesiastical year</a>, until the preparation for the next <a href="/wiki/Great_Lent" title="Great Lent">Great Lent</a>, are named for the day after Pentecost on which they occur (for example, the 13th Tuesday After Pentecost). </p>
      <p>The Second Monday after Pentecost is the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/Apostles%27_Fast" title="Apostles' Fast">Apostles' Fast</a> (which continues until the <a href="/wiki/Feast_of_Saints_Peter_and_Paul" title="Feast of Saints Peter and Paul">Feast of Saints Peter and Paul</a> on June 29). Theologically, Orthodox do not consider Pentecost to be the "birthday" of the Church; they see the Church as having existed before the creation of the world (cf. <i><a href="/wiki/The_Shepherd_of_Hermas" title="The Shepherd of Hermas">The Shepherd of Hermas</a></i>)<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[45]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The Orthodox <a href="/wiki/Icon" title="Icon">icon</a> of the feast depicts the Twelve Apostles seated in a semicircle (sometimes the <a href="/wiki/Theotokos" title="Theotokos">Theotokos</a> (Virgin Mary) is shown sitting in the center of them). At the top of the icon, the Holy Spirit, in the form of tongues of fire, is descending upon them. At the bottom is an <a href="/wiki/Allegory" title="Allegory">allegorical</a> figure, called <i><a href="/wiki/Cosmos" title="Cosmos">Kosmos</a></i>, which symbolizes the world. Although Kosmos is crowned with earthly glory he sits in the darkness caused by the ignorance of God. He is holding a towel on which have been placed 12 scrolls, representing the teaching of the Twelve Apostles. </p>
      <p>In the ancient <a href="/wiki/Coptic_Orthodox_Church_of_Alexandria" title="Coptic Orthodox Church of Alexandria">Coptic Orthodox Church of Alexandria</a>, Pentecost is one of the seven Major "Lord's Feasts". It is celebrated at the time of ninth hour (3:00&nbsp;pm) on the Sunday of Pentecost by a special three-segment prayer known as the "Office of Genuflection (Kneeling Prayer)". This feast is followed with the "Apostles Fast" which has a fixed end date on the fifth of the Coptic month of Epip [which currently falls on July 12, which is equivalent to June 29, due to the current 13-day Julian-Gregorian calendar offset]. The fifth of Epip is the commemoration of the Martyrdom of St. Peter and Paul. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Western_churches">Western churches</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pentecost&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Western churches">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Pentecost_Altar.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Pentecost_Altar.jpg/220px-Pentecost_Altar.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Pentecost_Altar.jpg/330px-Pentecost_Altar.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Pentecost_Altar.jpg/440px-Pentecost_Altar.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2560" data-file-height="1920"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Pentecost_Altar.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>A Protestant church altar, decorated for Pentecost with red burning candles and red banners and altar cloth depicting the movement of the Holy Spirit
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The liturgical celebrations of Pentecost in <a href="/wiki/Western_Christianity" title="Western Christianity">Western churches</a> are as rich and varied as those in the East. The main sign of Pentecost in the West is the color red. It symbolizes joy and the fire of the Holy Spirit. </p>
      <p>Priests or ministers, and choirs wear red <a href="/wiki/Vestments" class="mw-redirect" title="Vestments">vestments</a>, and in modern times, the custom has extended to the <a href="/wiki/Laity" title="Laity">lay people</a> of the congregation wearing red clothing in celebration as well. Red banners are often hung from walls or ceilings to symbolize the blowing of the "mighty wind"<sup id="cite_ref-bibleverse|Acts|2:2|KJV_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bibleverse|Acts|2:2|KJV-49">[46]</a></sup> and the free movement of the Spirit.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[47]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The celebrations may depict symbols of the Holy Spirit, such as the dove or flames, symbols of the church such as <a href="/wiki/Noah%27s_Ark" title="Noah's Ark">Noah's Ark</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Pomegranate" title="Pomegranate">Pomegranate</a>, or especially within Protestant churches of <a href="/wiki/Reformed" class="mw-redirect" title="Reformed">Reformed</a> and <a href="/wiki/Evangelicalism" title="Evangelicalism">Evangelical</a> traditions, words rather than images naming for example, the <a href="/wiki/Seven_gifts_of_the_Holy_Spirit" title="Seven gifts of the Holy Spirit">gifts</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fruits_of_the_Spirit" class="mw-redirect" title="Fruits of the Spirit">Fruits of the Spirit</a>. Red flowers at the altar/preaching area, and red flowering plants such as <a href="/wiki/Geraniums" class="mw-redirect" title="Geraniums">geraniums</a> around the church are also typical decorations for Pentecost masses/services. These symbolize the renewal of life, the coming of the warmth of summer, and the growth of the church at and from the first Pentecost.<sup id="cite_ref-stcatherineofsweden1_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stcatherineofsweden1-51">[48]</a></sup> In the southern hemisphere, for example, in southern Australia, Pentecost comes in the mellow autumntide, after the often great heat of summer, and the red leaves of the poinsettia have often been used to decorate churches then. </p> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Protestant_Altar_Pentecost_Red_Flowers_Green_Birch.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A Protestant church altar and font, decorated for Pentecost with red flowering plants and green birch branches" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Protestant_Altar_Pentecost_Red_Flowers_Green_Birch.jpg/220px-Protestant_Altar_Pentecost_Red_Flowers_Green_Birch.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Protestant_Altar_Pentecost_Red_Flowers_Green_Birch.jpg/330px-Protestant_Altar_Pentecost_Red_Flowers_Green_Birch.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Protestant_Altar_Pentecost_Red_Flowers_Green_Birch.jpg/440px-Protestant_Altar_Pentecost_Red_Flowers_Green_Birch.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3264" data-file-height="2448"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Protestant_Altar_Pentecost_Red_Flowers_Green_Birch.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>A Protestant church altar and font, decorated for Pentecost with red flowering plants and green birch branches
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>These flowers often play an important role in the ancestral rites, and other rites, of the particular congregation. For example, in both Protestant and Catholic churches, the plants brought in to decorate for the holiday may be each "sponsored" by individuals in memory of a particular loved one, or in honor of a living person on a significant occasion, such as their <a href="/wiki/Confirmation" title="Confirmation">Confirmation</a> day.<sup id="cite_ref-stcatherineofsweden1_51-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stcatherineofsweden1-51">[48]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In <a href="/wiki/German_language_in_Europe" class="mw-redirect" title="German language in Europe">German speaking</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Central_Europe" title="Central Europe">Central European</a> countries, and also in overseas congregations originating from these countries through migration, green branches are also traditionally used to decorate churches for Pentecost. <a href="/wiki/Birch" title="Birch">Birch</a> is the tree most typically associated with this practice in Europe, but other species are employed in different climates.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>The singing of Pentecost hymns is also central to the celebration in the Western tradition. Hymns such as <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther" title="Martin Luther">Martin Luther</a>'s "<a href="/wiki/Komm,_Heiliger_Geist,_Herre_Gott" title="Komm, Heiliger Geist, Herre Gott">Komm, Heiliger Geist, Herre Gott</a>" (Come, Holy Spirit, God and Lord),<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[49]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[50]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Charles_Wesley" title="Charles Wesley">Charles Wesley</a>'s "Spirit of Faith Come Down"<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[51]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[52]</a></sup> and "Come Holy Ghost Our Hearts Inspire"<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[53]</a></sup> or <a href="/wiki/Hildegard_von_Bingen" class="mw-redirect" title="Hildegard von Bingen">Hildegard von Bingen</a>'s "O Holy Spirit Root of Life"<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[54]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[55]</a></sup> are popular. Some traditional hymns of Pentecost make reference not only to themes relating to the Holy Spirit or the church, but to folk customs connected to the holiday as well, such as the decorating with green branches.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[56]</a></sup> Other hymns include "Oh that I had a Thousand Voices" ("<i>O daß ich tausend Zungen hätte</i>")<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[57]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[58]</a></sup> by German, Johann Mentzer Verse 2: "<i>Ye forest leaves so green and tender, that dance for joy in summer air</i>…" or "O Day Full of Grace" ("<i>Den signede Dag</i>")<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[59]</a></sup> by <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Denmark" class="mw-redirect" title="Kingdom of Denmark">Dane</a>, <a href="/wiki/N._F._S._Grundtvig" title="N. F. S. Grundtvig">N. F. S. Grundtvig</a> verse 3: "<i>Yea were every tree endowed with speech and every leaflet singing</i>…". </p>
      <p>As Pentecost closes the Easter Season in the Roman Catholic Church, the dismissal with the double alleluia is sung at the end of Mass.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[60]</a></sup> The Paschal Candle is removed from the sanctuary at the end of the day. In the Roman Catholic Church, <a href="/wiki/Veni_Sancte_Spiritus" title="Veni Sancte Spiritus">Veni Sancte Spiritus</a> is the <a href="/wiki/Sequence_(poetry)" class="mw-redirect" title="Sequence (poetry)">sequence hymn</a> for the Day of Pentecost. This has been translated into many languages and is sung in many denominations today. As an invocation of the Holy Spirit, <a href="/wiki/Veni_Creator_Spiritus" title="Veni Creator Spiritus">Veni Creator Spiritus</a> is sung during liturgical celebrations on the feast of Pentecost.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[61]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[62]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Trumpeters or <a href="/wiki/Brass_instrument" title="Brass instrument">brass ensembles</a> are often specially contracted to accompany singing and provide special music at Pentecost services, recalling the Sound of the mighty wind.<sup id="cite_ref-bibleverse|Acts|2:2|KJV_49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bibleverse|Acts|2:2|KJV-49">[46]</a></sup> While this practice is common among a wide spectrum of Western denominations (Eastern Churches do not employ instrumental accompaniment in their worship) it is particularly typical, and distinctive to the heritage of the <a href="/wiki/Moravian_Church_music" title="Moravian Church music">Moravian</a> Church.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[63]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:St.Peter_und_Paul_in_S%C3%B6ll_-_Heilig-Geist-Loch.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/St.Peter_und_Paul_in_S%C3%B6ll_-_Heilig-Geist-Loch.jpg/220px-St.Peter_und_Paul_in_S%C3%B6ll_-_Heilig-Geist-Loch.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/St.Peter_und_Paul_in_S%C3%B6ll_-_Heilig-Geist-Loch.jpg/330px-St.Peter_und_Paul_in_S%C3%B6ll_-_Heilig-Geist-Loch.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/St.Peter_und_Paul_in_S%C3%B6ll_-_Heilig-Geist-Loch.jpg/440px-St.Peter_und_Paul_in_S%C3%B6ll_-_Heilig-Geist-Loch.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2560" data-file-height="1712"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:St.Peter_und_Paul_in_S%C3%B6ll_-_Heilig-Geist-Loch.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Holy Ghost hole, Saints Peter and Paul Church in <a href="/wiki/S%C3%B6ll" title="Söll">Söll</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Another custom is reading the appointed Scripture lessons in multiple foreign languages recounting the speaking in tongues recorded in <span class="nourlexpansion nowrap"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Acts+2%3A4%E2%80%9312&amp;version=NRSV">Acts 2:4–12</a></span>.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[64]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>, cathedrals and great churches throughout Western Europe were fitted with a peculiar architectural feature known as a Holy Ghost hole: a small circular opening in the roof that symbolized the entrance of the Holy Spirit into the midst of the congregation. At Pentecost, these Holy Ghost holes would be decorated with flowers, and sometimes a dove figure lowered through into the church while the narrative of Pentecost was read. Holy Ghost holes can still be seen today in European churches such as <a href="/wiki/Canterbury_Cathedral" title="Canterbury Cathedral">Canterbury Cathedral</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="I can't find any scholarly references to this – 'Middle Ages' is a huge category with ecclesiastical and architectural shifts occurring in various countries at various times. (November 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>Similarly, a large two dimensional dove figure would be, and in some places still is, cut from wood, painted, and decorated with flowers, to be lowered over the congregation, particularly during the singing of the <a href="/wiki/Sequence_(poetry)" class="mw-redirect" title="Sequence (poetry)">sequence hymn</a>, or <i><a href="/wiki/Veni_Creator_Spiritus" title="Veni Creator Spiritus">Veni Creator Spiritus</a></i>. In other places, particularly <a href="/wiki/Sicily" title="Sicily">Sicily</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italian peninsula</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rosalia_(festival)#Rose_Sundays" title="Rosalia (festival)">rose petals</a> were and are thrown from the galleries over the congregation, recalling the tongues of fire. In modern times, this practice has been revived, and adapted as well, to include the strewing of <a href="/wiki/Origami" title="Origami">origami</a> doves from above or suspending them, sometimes by the hundreds, from the ceiling.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[65]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In some cases, red fans, or red handkerchiefs, are distributed to the congregation to be waved during the procession, etc. Other congregations have incorporated the use of red balloons, signifying the "Birthday of the Church". These may be borne by the congregants, decorate the sanctuary, or released all at once. </p> 
      <h3><span id="Fasting.2C_baptisms.2C_and_confirmations"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Fasting,_baptisms,_and_confirmations">Fasting, baptisms, and confirmations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pentecost&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Fasting, baptisms, and confirmations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>For some <a href="/wiki/Protestants" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestants">Protestants</a>, the nine days between <a href="/wiki/Ascension_Day" class="mw-redirect" title="Ascension Day">Ascension Day</a>, and Pentecost are set aside as a time of <a href="/wiki/Fasting" title="Fasting">fasting</a> and universal prayer in honor of the disciples' time of prayer and unity awaiting the Holy Spirit. Similarly among <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholics" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholics">Roman Catholics</a>, special Pentecost <a href="/wiki/Novenas" class="mw-redirect" title="Novenas">novenas</a> are prayed. The Pentecost Novena is considered the first novena, all other novenas prayed in preparation of various feasts deriving their practice from those original nine days of prayer observed by the disciples of Christ. </p>
      <p>While the Eve of Pentecost was traditionally a day of fasting for Catholics, contemporary <a href="/wiki/Canon_law" title="Canon law">canon law</a> no longer requires it. Both Catholics and Protestants may hold spiritual <a href="/wiki/Retreats" class="mw-redirect" title="Retreats">retreats</a>, prayer <a href="/wiki/Vigil" title="Vigil">vigils</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Litanies" class="mw-redirect" title="Litanies">litanies</a> in the days leading up to Pentecost. In some cases vigils on the Eve of Pentecost may last all night. Pentecost is also one of the occasions specially appointed for the Lutheran Litany to be sung.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[66]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>From the early days of Western Christianity, Pentecost became one of the days set aside to celebrate <a href="/wiki/Baptism" title="Baptism">Baptism</a>. In Northern Europe Pentecost was preferred even over Easter for this rite, as the temperatures in late spring might be supposed to be more conducive to outdoor <a href="/wiki/Immersion_baptism" title="Immersion baptism">immersion</a> as was then the practice. It is proposed that the term <a href="/wiki/Whit_Sunday" class="mw-redirect" title="Whit Sunday">Whit Sunday</a> derives from the custom of the newly baptized wearing white clothing, and from the white vestments worn by the clergy in <a href="/wiki/Sarum_Rite" class="mw-redirect" title="Sarum Rite">English liturgical uses</a>. The holiday was also one of the three days each year (along with <a href="/wiki/Christmas" title="Christmas">Christmas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Easter" title="Easter">Easter</a>) Roman Catholics were required to <a href="/wiki/Confession_(religion)" title="Confession (religion)">confess</a> and receive <a href="/wiki/Holy_Communion" class="mw-redirect" title="Holy Communion">Holy Communion</a> in order to remain in good ecclesiastical standing.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[67]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Holy Communion is likewise often a feature of the Protestant observance of Pentecost as well. It is one of the relatively few Sundays some <a href="/wiki/Reformed" class="mw-redirect" title="Reformed">Reformed</a> denominations may offer the communion meal, and is one of the days of the year specially appointed among <a href="/wiki/Moravian_Church" title="Moravian Church">Moravians</a> for the celebration of their Love Feasts. <a href="/wiki/Ordinations" class="mw-redirect" title="Ordinations">Ordinations</a> are celebrated across a wide array of Western denominations at Pentecost, or near to it. In some denominations, for example the <a href="/wiki/Lutheran" class="mw-redirect" title="Lutheran">Lutheran</a> Church, even if an ordination or consecration of a <a href="/wiki/Deaconess" title="Deaconess">deaconess</a> is not celebrated on Pentecost, the <a href="/wiki/Liturgical_color" class="mw-redirect" title="Liturgical color">liturgical color</a> will invariably be red, and the theme of the service will be the Holy Spirit. </p>
      <p>Above all, Pentecost is a day to hold <a href="/wiki/Confirmation" title="Confirmation">Confirmation</a> celebrations for youth. Flowers, the wearing of white robes or white dresses recalling Baptism, rites such as the <a href="/wiki/Laying_on_of_hands" title="Laying on of hands">laying on of hands</a>, and vibrant singing play prominent roles on these joyous occasions, the blossoming of Spring forming an equal analogy with the blossoming of youth. </p>
      <p>The typical image of Pentecost in the West is that of the <a href="/wiki/Virgin_Mary" class="mw-redirect" title="Virgin Mary">Virgin Mary</a> seated centrally and prominently among the disciples with flames resting on the crowns of their heads. Occasionally, parting clouds suggesting the action of the "mighty wind",<sup id="cite_ref-bibleverse|Acts|2:2|KJV_49-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bibleverse|Acts|2:2|KJV-49">[46]</a></sup> rays of light and the Dove are also depicted. Of course, the Western iconographic style is less static and stylized than that of the East, and other very different representations have been produced, and, in some cases, have achieved great fame such as the Pentecosts by <a href="/wiki/Titian" title="Titian">Titian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Giotto" title="Giotto">Giotto</a>, and <a href="/wiki/El_Greco" title="El Greco">el Greco</a>. </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/Paul_of_Tarsus" class="mw-redirect" title="Paul of Tarsus">St. Paul</a> already in the 1st century notes the importance of this festival to the <a href="/wiki/Early_Christian" class="mw-redirect" title="Early Christian">early Christian</a> communities. (See: <span class="nourlexpansion nowrap"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Acts+20%3A16&amp;version=NRSV">Acts 20:16</a></span> &amp; <span class="nourlexpansion nowrap"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=1+Corinthians+16%3A8&amp;version=NRSV">1 Corinthians 16:8</a></span>) Since the lifetime of some who may have been eyewitnesses, annual celebrations of the descent of the Holy Spirit have been observed. Before the <a href="/wiki/Second_Vatican_Council" title="Second Vatican Council">Second Vatican Council</a> <a href="/wiki/Whitmonday" class="mw-redirect" title="Whitmonday">Pentecost Monday</a> as well was a <a href="/wiki/Holy_Day_of_Obligation" class="mw-redirect" title="Holy Day of Obligation">Holy Day of Obligation</a> during which the Catholic Church addressed the newly baptized and confirmed. After the council, Pentecost Monday is no longer solemnized. </p>
      <p>Nevertheless, Pentecost Monday remains an official festival in many Protestant churches, such as the (Lutheran) <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Sweden" title="Church of Sweden">Church of Sweden</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Evangelical_Lutheran_Church_of_Finland" title="Evangelical Lutheran Church of Finland">Evangelical Lutheran Church of Finland</a>, and others. In the <a href="/wiki/Greek_Byzantine_Catholic_Church" title="Greek Byzantine Catholic Church">Byzantine Catholic Rite</a> Pentecost Monday is no longer a <a href="/wiki/Holy_Day_of_Obligation" class="mw-redirect" title="Holy Day of Obligation">Holy Day of Obligation</a>, but rather a simple <a href="/wiki/Holiday" title="Holiday">holiday</a>. In the Extraordinary Form of the liturgy of the Roman Catholic Church, as at Easter, the <a href="/wiki/Ranks_of_Catholic_liturgical_days" class="mw-redirect" title="Ranks of Catholic liturgical days">liturgical rank</a> of Monday and Tuesday of Pentecost week is a Double of the First Class<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[68]</a></sup> and across many Western denominations, Pentecost is celebrated with an <a href="/wiki/Octave_(liturgical)" class="mw-redirect" title="Octave (liturgical)">octave</a> culminating on <a href="/wiki/Trinity_Sunday" title="Trinity Sunday">Trinity Sunday</a>. However, in the modern Roman Rite (Ordinary Form), Pentecost ends after Evening Prayer on the feast day itself, with <a href="/wiki/Ordinary_Time" title="Ordinary Time">Ordinary Time</a> resuming the next day. </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Duccio_di_Buoninsegna_018.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A typical Western image of the Pentecost. Duccio di Buoninsegna (1308) Tempera on wood." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Duccio_di_Buoninsegna_018.jpg/220px-Duccio_di_Buoninsegna_018.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="195" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Duccio_di_Buoninsegna_018.jpg/330px-Duccio_di_Buoninsegna_018.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Duccio_di_Buoninsegna_018.jpg/440px-Duccio_di_Buoninsegna_018.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2024" data-file-height="1796"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Duccio_di_Buoninsegna_018.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>A typical Western image of the Pentecost. <a href="/wiki/Duccio_di_Buoninsegna" class="mw-redirect" title="Duccio di Buoninsegna">Duccio di Buoninsegna</a> (1308)
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Pentecost_01.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Pentecost_01.jpg/220px-Pentecost_01.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="245" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Pentecost_01.jpg/330px-Pentecost_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Pentecost_01.jpg/440px-Pentecost_01.jpg 2x" data-file-width="897" data-file-height="1000"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Pentecost_01.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The Pentecost depicted in a 14th-century <a href="/wiki/Missal" title="Missal">Missal</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Marking the festival's importance, in several denominations, such as the Lutheran, Episcopal, and <a href="/wiki/United_Methodist" class="mw-redirect" title="United Methodist">United Methodist</a> churches, and formerly in the Roman Catholic Church, all the Sundays from the holiday itself until <a href="/wiki/Advent" title="Advent">Advent</a> in late November or December are designated the 2nd, 3rd, Nth, Sunday after Pentecost, etc. Throughout the year, in Roman Catholic piety, Pentecost is the third of the <a href="/wiki/Glorious_Mysteries" class="mw-redirect" title="Glorious Mysteries">Glorious Mysteries</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Rosary" title="Rosary">Holy Rosary</a>, as well as being one of the <a href="/wiki/Stations_of_the_Resurrection" title="Stations of the Resurrection">Stations of the Resurrection</a> or Via Lucis. </p>
      <p>In some <a href="/wiki/Evangelicalism" title="Evangelicalism">Evangelical</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pentecostal" class="mw-redirect" title="Pentecostal">Pentecostal</a> churches, where there is less emphasis on the <a href="/wiki/Liturgical_year" title="Liturgical year">liturgical year</a>, Pentecost may still be one of the greatest celebrations in the year, such as in <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a> or <a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">Romania</a>. In other cases, Pentecost may be ignored as a holy day in these churches. In many evangelical churches in the United States, the secular holiday, <a href="/wiki/Mother%27s_Day" title="Mother's Day">Mother's Day</a>, may be more celebrated than the ancient and biblical feast of Pentecost.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[69]</a></sup> Some evangelicals and Pentecostals are observing the liturgical calendar and observe Pentecost as a day to teach the <a href="/wiki/Gifts_of_the_Holy_Spirit" class="mw-redirect" title="Gifts of the Holy Spirit">Gifts of the Holy Spirit</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (December 2012)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>Across denominational lines Pentecost has been an opportunity for Christians to honor the role of the Holy Spirit in their lives, and celebrate the birth of the Church in an <a href="/wiki/Ecumenical" class="mw-redirect" title="Ecumenical">ecumenical</a> context.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[70]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[71]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mariology">Mariology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pentecost&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Mariology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <p>A secular iconography in the Western and in the Eastern Churches reflect the belief of the presence of the Blessed Virgin Mary in day of Pentecost and her central role on the divine concession of the gift of the Holy Spirit God to the Apostles. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://bible.oremus.org/?passage=Acts%201:12&amp;version=nrsv">Acts 1.12</a> confirms the presence of the mother of Jesus with the Twelve in a spiritual communion of daily prayer. It is the unique reference to the Mother of God after Jesus' entrusting to St. John the Evangelist and the Apostle at the feet of the cross, an episode known as <i><a href="/wiki/Ecce_homo" title="Ecce homo">Ecce homo</a></i>. </p>
      <p>According to that iconographic tradition, the Latin encyclical <i><a href="/wiki/Mystici_Corporis_Christi" class="mw-redirect" title="Mystici Corporis Christi">Mystici Corporis Christi</a></i> officially stated: </p> 
      <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style>
      <blockquote class="templatequote">
       <p>She it was through her powerful prayers obtained that the spirit of our Divine Redeemer, already given on the Cross, should be bestowed, accompanied by miraculous gifts, on the newly founded Church at Pentecost; and finally, bearing with courage and confidence the tremendous burden of her sorrows and desolation, she, truly the Queen of Martyrs, more than all the faithful "filled up those things that are wanting of the sufferings of Christ...for His Body, which is the Church"; and she continues to have for the Mystical Body of Christ, born of the pierced Heart of the Savior, the same motherly care and ardent love with which she cherished and fed the Infant Jesus in the crib. </p>
       <div class="templatequotecite">
        — <cite><a href="/wiki/Pope_Pius_XII" title="Pope Pius XII">Pope Pius XII</a>, Mystici Corporis Christi, 2 March 1943<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[72]</a></sup></cite>
       </div>
      </blockquote> 
      <p>The Roman Catholic and the Orthodox Church recognize to the Mother of God a special form of veneration called with the Greek word <i><a href="/wiki/Veneration#Catholicism_and_Orthodoxy" title="Veneration">hyperdulia</a></i>. It corresponds to a special power of <a href="/wiki/Intercession_of_saints" title="Intercession of saints">intercession</a> that the prayers dedicated to the Most Blessed Virgin Mary have in respect of any other saint praying God in favour of His sons and believers. The Pope stated that Mary prayed God and her prayer of intercession was capable to persuade God to send His Holy Spirit as a permanent gift for the Twelve and their successors forming the Apostolic Church. </p>
      <p>In a similar way, Pope John Paul II in the general audience held in Vatican on May 28, 1997, affirmed: </p> 
      <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942">
      <blockquote class="templatequote">
       <p>Retracing the course of the Virgin Mary’s life, the Second Vatican Council recalls her presence in the community waiting for Pentecost. “But since it had pleased God not to manifest solemnly the mystery of the salvation of the human race before he would pour forth the Spirit promised by Christ, we see the Apostles before the day of Pentecost ‘persevering with one mind in prayer with the women and Mary the Mother of Jesus, and with his brethren’ (Acts 1:14), and we also see Mary by her prayers imploring the gift of the Spirit, who had already overshadowed her in the Annunciation” (<a href="/wiki/Lumen_gentium" title="Lumen gentium">Lumen gentium</a>, n.59). The first community is the prelude to the birth of the Church; the Blessed Virgin’s presence helps to sketch her definitive features, a fruit of the gift of Pentecost. [...] </p>
       <p>In contemplating Mary’s powerful intercession as she waits for the Holy Spirit, Christians of every age have frequently had recourse to her intercession on the long and tiring journey to salvation, in order to receive the gifts of the Paraclete in greater abundance. [...] </p>
       <p> In the Church and for the Church, mindful of Jesus’ promise, she waits for Pentecost and implores a multiplicity of gifts for everyone, in accordance with each one's personality and mission.</p>
       <div class="templatequotecite">
        — <cite>Pope John Paul II, <i>General Audience</i>, 28 May 1997, Rome<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[73]</a></sup></cite>
       </div>
      </blockquote> 
      <p>The Marian intercessory prayer is dated to the day before the Pentecost, while it isn't explicitly stated her presence with the Apostles in the day of the Solemnity, in consideration of that she was called "full of grace" of the Holy Spirit God since the time of the <a href="/wiki/Annunciation" title="Annunciation">Annunciation</a>. </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Music">Music</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pentecost&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Music">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <p><a href="/wiki/List_of_hymns_for_Pentecost" title="List of hymns for Pentecost">Several hymns were written and composed for Pentecost</a>, notably <a href="/wiki/Veni_Creator_Spiritus" title="Veni Creator Spiritus">Veni Creator Spiritus</a> (Come, Creator Spirit), attributed to the 9th-century <a href="/wiki/Rabanus_Maurus" title="Rabanus Maurus">Rabanus Maurus</a>, and translated throughout the centuries in different languages. This one and some more are suitable also for other occasions imploring the Holy Spirit, such as <a href="/wiki/Ordination_in_the_Catholic_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="Ordination in the Catholic Church">ordinations</a> and coronations. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Classical_compositions">Classical compositions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pentecost&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Classical compositions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <table class="box-More_citations_needed plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation">
       <tbody>
        <tr>
         <td class="mbox-image">
          <div style="width:52px">
           <a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" decoding="async" width="50" height="39" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399"></a>
          </div></td>
         <td class="mbox-text">
          <div class="mbox-text-span">
           This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Pentecost&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Referencing_for_beginners" title="Help:Referencing for beginners">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.<br><small><span class="plainlinks"><i>Find sources:</i>&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?as_eq=wikipedia&amp;q=%22Pentecost%22">"Pentecost"</a>&nbsp;–&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?tbm=nws&amp;q=%22Pentecost%22+-wikipedia">news</a>&nbsp;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?&amp;q=%22Pentecost%22+site:news.google.com/newspapers&amp;source=newspapers">newspapers</a>&nbsp;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?tbs=bks:1&amp;q=%22Pentecost%22+-wikipedia">books</a>&nbsp;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//scholar.google.com/scholar?q=%22Pentecost%22">scholar</a>&nbsp;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.jstor.org/action/doBasicSearch?Query=%22Pentecost%22&amp;acc=on&amp;wc=on">JSTOR</a></span></small></span> <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">May 2018</span>)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small>
          </div></td>
        </tr>
       </tbody>
      </table> 
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Lutheranism" title="Lutheranism">Lutheran</a> church of the <a href="/wiki/Baroque" title="Baroque">Baroque</a> observed three days of Pentecost. Some composers wrote sacred <a href="/wiki/Cantata" title="Cantata">cantatas</a> to be performed in the church services of these days. <a href="/wiki/Johann_Sebastian_Bach" title="Johann Sebastian Bach">Johann Sebastian Bach</a> composed several <a href="/wiki/Bach_cantata" title="Bach cantata">cantatas</a> for <a href="/wiki/Church_cantata#Pentecost" title="Church cantata">Pentecost</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Erschallet,_ihr_Lieder,_erklinget,_ihr_Saiten!_BWV_172" title="Erschallet, ihr Lieder, erklinget, ihr Saiten! BWV 172"><i>Erschallet, ihr Lieder, erklinget, ihr Saiten!</i> BWV 172</a>, in 1714 and <a href="/wiki/Also_hat_Gott_die_Welt_geliebt,_BWV_68" title="Also hat Gott die Welt geliebt, BWV 68"><i>Also hat Gott die Welt geliebt</i>, BWV 68</a>, in 1725. <a href="/wiki/Gottfried_Heinrich_St%C3%B6lzel" title="Gottfried Heinrich Stölzel">Gottfried Heinrich Stölzel</a> wrote cantatas such as <i>Werdet voll Geistes</i> (Get full of spirit) in 1737.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[74]</a></sup> Mozart composed an antiphon <i><a href="/wiki/Veni_Sancte_Spiritus_(Mozart)" title="Veni Sancte Spiritus (Mozart)">Veni Sancte Spiritus</a></i> in 1768. </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/Olivier_Messiaen" title="Olivier Messiaen">Olivier Messiaen</a> composed an organ mass <i><a href="/wiki/Messe_de_la_Pentec%C3%B4te" title="Messe de la Pentecôte">Messe de la Pentecôte</a></i> in 1949/50. In 1964 <a href="/wiki/Fritz_Werner" title="Fritz Werner">Fritz Werner</a> wrote an <a href="/wiki/Oratorio" title="Oratorio">oratorio</a> for Pentecost <i>Veni, sancte spiritus</i> (Come, Holy Spirit) on the sequence <i><a href="/wiki/Veni_Sancte_Spiritus" title="Veni Sancte Spiritus">Veni Sancte Spiritus</a></i>, and <a href="/wiki/Jani_Christou" title="Jani Christou">Jani Christou</a> wrote <i>Tongues of Fire</i>, a Pentecost oratorio. <a href="/wiki/Richard_Hillert" title="Richard Hillert">Richard Hillert</a> wrote a <i>Motet for the Day of Pentecost</i> for choir, vibraphone, and prepared electronic tape in 1969. <a href="/wiki/Violeta_Dinescu" title="Violeta Dinescu">Violeta Dinescu</a> composed <i>Pfingstoratorium</i>, an oratorio for Pentecost for five soloists, mixed chorus and small orchestra in 1993. Daniel Elder's 21st century piece, "Factus est Repente", for a cappella choir, was premiered in 2013. </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Customs_and_traditions">Customs and traditions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pentecost&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Customs and traditions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <p>In <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a> it was customary to scatter <a href="/wiki/Rose" title="Rose">rose</a> petals from the ceiling of the churches to recall the miracle of the fiery tongues; hence in <a href="/wiki/Sicily" title="Sicily">Sicily</a> and elsewhere in Italy Whitsunday is called <i>Pasqua rosatum</i>. The <a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">Italian</a> name <i>Pasqua rossa</i> comes from the red colours of the vestments used on Whitsunday.<sup id="cite_ref-Kellner_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kellner-78">[75]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a> it was customary to blow <a href="/wiki/Trumpet" title="Trumpet">trumpets</a> during Divine service, to recall the sound of the mighty wind which accompanied the Descent of the Holy Spirit.<sup id="cite_ref-Kellner_78-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kellner-78">[75]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In the north west of <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a>, church and chapel parades called Whit Walks take place at Whitsun (sometimes on Whit Friday, the Friday after Whitsun).<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[76]</a></sup> Typically, the parades contain brass bands and choirs; girls attending are dressed in white. Traditionally, Whit Fairs (sometimes called Whitsun Ales)<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[77]</a></sup> took place. Other customs such as <a href="/wiki/Morris_dancing" class="mw-redirect" title="Morris dancing">morris dancing</a><sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[78]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Cheese_rolling" class="mw-redirect" title="Cheese rolling">cheese rolling</a><sup id="cite_ref-BBC2005_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC2005-82">[79]</a></sup> are also associated with Whitsun. "Whitsunday" has been the name of the day in the Church of England. (The Book of Common Prayer only once uses the word "Pentecost" for the festival. Though some<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (May 2015)">who?</span></a></i>]</sup> think that name derives from white clothes worn by newly baptised in Eastertide, it may well be seen as derived from "wit", hence "wisdom", the reference being to Holy Wisdom (Sancta Sophia, Hagia Sophia), referred to in Proverbs and the Book of Wisdom, with which the Holy Spirit has often been identified. </p>
      <p>In <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a> there is a saying known virtually by everyone which translates as "if one has no sweetheart until Pentecost, he/she will not have it during the whole summer."<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[80]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In <a href="/wiki/Port_Vila" title="Port Vila">Port Vila</a>, the capital of <a href="/wiki/Vanuatu" title="Vanuatu">Vanuatu</a>, people originating from <a href="/wiki/Pentecost_Island" title="Pentecost Island">Pentecost Island</a> usually celebrate their island's name-day with a special church service followed by cultural events such as dancing.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>In <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukraine</a> the springtime feast day of <i>Zeleni Sviata</i> became associated with the Pentecost. (The exact origin of the relationship is not known). The customs for the festival were performed in the following order: first, home and <a href="/wiki/Hearth" title="Hearth">hearth</a> would be cleaned; second, foods were prepared for the festival; finally, homes and churches were decorated with wildflowers and various types of green <a href="/wiki/Herbs" class="mw-redirect" title="Herbs">herbs</a> and plants. A seven course meal may have been served as the Pentecost feast which may have included traditional dishes such as <a href="/wiki/Cereal" title="Cereal">cereal</a> with <a href="/wiki/Honey" title="Honey">honey</a> (<i>kolyvo</i>), <a href="/wiki/Rice" title="Rice">rice</a> or <a href="/wiki/Millet" title="Millet">millet</a> grains with <a href="/wiki/Milk" title="Milk">milk</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sauerkraut" title="Sauerkraut">sauerkraut</a> soup (<i>kapusniak</i>), chicken broth with handmade <a href="/wiki/Noodle" title="Noodle">noodles</a> (<i>iushka z zaterkoiu</i>), cheese turnovers (<i>pyrizhky syrom</i>), roast pork, <a href="/wiki/Buckwheat" title="Buckwheat">buckwheat</a> cakes served with <a href="/wiki/Egg_as_food" title="Egg as food">eggs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cheese" title="Cheese">cheese</a> (<i>blyntsi</i>), and baked <a href="/wiki/Kasha" title="Kasha">kasha</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[81]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Date_and_public_holiday">Date and public holiday</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pentecost&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Date and public holiday">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <p>The earliest possible date is May 10 (as in 1818 and 2285). The latest possible date is June 13 (as in 1943 and 2038). The day of Pentecost is seven weeks after Easter Sunday: that is to say, the fiftieth day after Easter inclusive of Easter Sunday.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[82]</a></sup> Pentecost may also refer to the 50 days from Easter to Pentecost Sunday inclusive of both.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[83]</a></sup> Because Easter itself has no fixed date, this makes Pentecost a moveable feast.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[84]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>While Eastern Christianity treats Pentecost as the last day of Easter in its liturgies, in the <a href="/wiki/Roman_liturgy" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman liturgy">Roman liturgy</a> it is usually a separate feast.<sup id="cite_ref-liturgy_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-liturgy-88">[85]</a></sup> The fifty days from Easter Sunday to Pentecost Sunday may also be called <a href="/wiki/Eastertide" title="Eastertide">Eastertide</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-liturgy_88-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-liturgy-88">[85]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Since Pentecost itself is on a Sunday, it is automatically considered to be a public holiday in countries with large Christian denominations. </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/Whit_Monday" title="Whit Monday">Pentecost Monday</a> is a public holiday in many countries including <a href="/wiki/Andorra" title="Andorra">Andorra</a>, <a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">Austria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">Belgium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Benin" title="Benin">Benin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a>, <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>, <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hungary" title="Hungary">Hungary</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iceland" title="Iceland">Iceland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Liechtenstein" title="Liechtenstein">Liechtenstein</a>, <a href="/wiki/Luxembourg" title="Luxembourg">Luxembourg</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a>, <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a>, <a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">Romania</a> (since 2008), <a href="/wiki/Senegal" title="Senegal">Senegal</a>, (most parts of) <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Switzerland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Togo" title="Togo">Togo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukraine</a>. </p>
      <p>In <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a> it was also a public holiday, but Pentecost Monday (Annandag Pingst) was replaced by Swedish National Day on June 6, by a government decision on December 15, 2004. In <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Malta" title="Malta">Malta</a>, it is no longer a public holiday. It was a public holiday in <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Ireland" title="Republic of Ireland">Ireland</a> until 1973, when it was replaced by Early Summer Holiday on the first Monday in June. In the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a> the day is known as <a href="/wiki/Whit_Monday" title="Whit Monday">Whit Monday</a>, and was a <a href="/wiki/Bank_holiday" title="Bank holiday">bank holiday</a> until 1967 when it was replaced by the Spring Bank Holiday on the last Monday in May. In France, following reactions to the implementation of the <i><a href="/wiki/Journ%C3%A9e_de_solidarit%C3%A9_envers_les_personnes_%C3%A2g%C3%A9es" title="Journée de solidarité envers les personnes âgées">Journée de solidarité envers les personnes âgées</a></i>, Pentecost Monday has been reestablished as a regular (not as a <i>working</i>) holiday on May 3, 2005.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[86]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Literary_allusions">Literary allusions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pentecost&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Literary allusions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <p>According to legend, <a href="/wiki/King_Arthur" title="King Arthur">King Arthur</a> always gathered all his knights at the round table for a feast and a quest on Pentecost: </p> 
      <blockquote> 
       <p>So ever the king had a custom that at the feast of Pentecost in especial, afore other feasts in the year, he would not go that day to meat until he had heard or seen of a great marvel. <sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[87]</a></sup> </p> 
      </blockquote> 
      <p>German poet <a href="/wiki/Johann_Wolfgang_von_Goethe" title="Johann Wolfgang von Goethe">Johann Wolfgang von Goethe</a> declared Pentecost "das liebliche Fest" – the lovely Feast, in a selection by the same name in his <i><a href="/wiki/Reineke_Fuchs" class="mw-redirect" title="Reineke Fuchs">Reineke Fuchs</a></i>. </p> 
      <dl>
       <dd>
        Pfingsten, das liebliche Fest, war gekommen;
       </dd> 
       <dd>
        es grünten und blühten Feld und Wald;
       </dd> 
       <dd>
        auf Hügeln und Höhn, in Büschen und Hecken
       </dd> 
       <dd>
        Übten ein fröhliches Lied die neuermunterten Vögel;
       </dd> 
       <dd>
        Jede Wiese sprosste von Blumen in duftenden Gründen,
       </dd> 
       <dd>
        Festlich heiter glänzte der Himmel und farbig die Erde.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[88]</a></sup>
       </dd>
      </dl> 
      <p>"Pfingsten, das liebliche Fest", speaks of Pentecost as a time of greening and blooming in fields, woods, hills, mountains, bushes and hedges, of birds singing new songs, meadows sprouting fragrant flowers, and of festive sunshine gleaming from the skies and coloring the earth – iconic lines idealizing the Pentecost holidays in the German-speaking lands. </p>
      <p>Further, Goethe records an old peasant proverb relating to Pentecost in his "Sankt-Rochus-Fest zu Bingen"<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[89]</a></sup> – <i>Ripe strawberries at Pentecost mean a good wine crop.</i> </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/Alexandre_Dumas,_p%C3%A8re" class="mw-redirect" title="Alexandre Dumas, père">Alexandre Dumas, père</a> mentions of Pentecost in <i><a href="/wiki/Twenty_Years_After" title="Twenty Years After">Twenty Years After</a></i> (French: Vingt ans après), the sequel to <i><a href="/wiki/The_Three_Musketeers" title="The Three Musketeers">The Three Musketeers</a></i>. A meal is planned for the holiday, to which La Ramée, second in command of the prison, is invited, and by which contrivance, the Duke is able to escape. He speaks sarcastically of the festival to his jailor, foreshadowing his escape&nbsp;: <i>"Now, what has Pentecost to do with me? Do you fear, say, that the Holy Ghost may come down in the form of fiery tongues and open the gates of my prison?"</i><sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[90]</a></sup> </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/William_Shakespeare" title="William Shakespeare">William Shakespeare</a> mentions Pentecost in a line from <i><a href="/wiki/Romeo_and_Juliet" title="Romeo and Juliet">Romeo and Juliet</a></i> Act 1, Scene V. At the ball at his home, Capulet speaks in refuting an overestimate of the time elapsed since he last danced: <i>"What, man? 'Tis not so much, 'tis not so much! 'Tis since the nuptial of Lucentio, Come Pentecost as quickly as it will, Some five-and-twenty years, and then we mask'd."</i><sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[91]</a></sup> Note here the allusion to the tradition of <a href="/wiki/Mumming" class="mw-redirect" title="Mumming">mumming</a>, <a href="/wiki/Morris_dancing" class="mw-redirect" title="Morris dancing">Morris dancing</a> and wedding celebrations at Pentecost. </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pentecost&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r936637989">.mw-parser-output .portal{border:solid #aaa 1px;padding:0}.mw-parser-output .portal.tleft{margin:0.5em 1em 0.5em 0}.mw-parser-output .portal.tright{margin:0.5em 0 0.5em 1em}.mw-parser-output .portal>ul{display:table;box-sizing:border-box;padding:0.1em;max-width:175px;background:#f9f9f9;font-size:85%;line-height:110%;font-style:italic;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .portal>ul>li{display:table-row}.mw-parser-output .portal>ul>li>span:first-child{display:table-cell;padding:0.2em;vertical-align:middle;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .portal>ul>li>span:last-child{display:table-cell;padding:0.2em 0.2em 0.2em 0.3em;vertical-align:middle}</style>
      <div role="navigation" aria-label="Portals" class="noprint portal plainlist tright"> 
       <ul> 
        <li><span><a href="/wiki/File:P_christianity.svg" class="image"><img alt="icon" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/P_christianity.svg/31px-P_christianity.svg.png" decoding="async" width="31" height="28" class="noviewer" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/P_christianity.svg/47px-P_christianity.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/P_christianity.svg/62px-P_christianity.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="360"></a></span><span><a href="/wiki/Portal:Christianity" title="Portal:Christianity">Christianity portal</a></span></li>

       <li><a href="/wiki/Seven_deacons" class="mw-redirect" title="Seven deacons">Seven deacons</a> (in Jerusalem and St. Philip in <a href="/wiki/Ashdod" title="Ashdod">Ashdod</a>)</li>
      </ul> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pentecost&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1011085734">.mw-parser-output .reflist{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em;list-style-type:decimal}.mw-parser-output .reflist .references{font-size:100%;margin-bottom:0;list-style-type:inherit}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns ol{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-alpha{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-roman{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-alpha{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-greek{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-roman{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style>
      <div class="reflist reflist-lower-roman"> 
       <div class="mw-references-wrap">
        <ol class="references"> 
         <li id="cite_note-5"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-5">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The Greek term used for Shavuot in the Septuagint translation of <a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/Deuteronomy#16:10" class="extiw" title="s:Bible (King James)/Deuteronomy">Deuteronomy 16:10</a> and <a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/Exodus#34:22" class="extiw" title="s:Bible (King James)/Exodus">Exodus 34:22</a> is <span lang="el" title="Greek-language text">ἑορτὴν ἑβδομάδων</span> (<i lang="grc-Latn" title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language romanization">heortēn hebdomdádōn</i>), often translated into English as "Festival of Weeks."<sup id="cite_ref-auto_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-3">[3]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup></span> </li> 
         <li id="cite_note-12"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-12">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">As part of the phrase <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text">ἐπ᾽ αὐτὴν ἔτους πεντηκοστοῦ καὶ ἑκατοστοῦ</span><sup id="cite_ref-acadbible1mac6.20_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-acadbible1mac6.20-11">[10]</a></sup>(<i lang="grc-Latn" title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language romanization">ep autēn etous pentēkastou kai hekatostou</i>, "in the hundred and fiftieth year", or some variation of the phrase in combination with other numbers to define a precise number of years, and sometimes months. See: "... in the hundred and fiftieth year..."<a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/1_Maccabees#6:20" class="extiw" title="s:Bible (King James)/1 Maccabees">1 Maccabees 6:20</a>, "In the hundred and one and fiftieth year..."<a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/1_Maccabees#7:1" class="extiw" title="s:Bible (King James)/1 Maccabees">1 Maccabees 7:1</a>, " Also the first month of the hundred fifty and second year..." <a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/1_Maccabees#9:3" class="extiw" title="s:Bible (King James)/1 Maccabees">1 Maccabees 9:3</a>, with other examples at <a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/1_Maccabees#9:54" class="extiw" title="s:Bible (King James)/1 Maccabees">1 Maccabees 9:54</a>, and <a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/2_Maccabees#14:4" class="extiw" title="s:Bible (King James)/2 Maccabees">2 Maccabees 14:4</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-TDNT_7-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TDNT-7">[6]</a></sup></span> </li> 
         <li id="cite_note-20"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-20">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">There is significant disagreement amongst modern scholars about the interpretation of "Judea" in Acts 2:9. <a href="/wiki/Tertullian" title="Tertullian">Tertullian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Augustine" class="mw-redirect" title="Augustine">Augustine</a> have rendered the "Judea" of Acts 2:9 as <i>Armeniam</i> (<a href="/wiki/Armenia" title="Armenia">Armenia</a>), while <a href="/wiki/Jerome" title="Jerome">Jerome</a> has called it <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a>, and <a href="/wiki/John_Chrysostom" title="John Chrysostom">John Chrysostom</a> has associated it with <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>. Additional possibilities including <a href="/wiki/Cilicia" title="Cilicia">Cilicia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ionia" title="Ionia">Ionia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lydia" title="Lydia">Lydia</a> have been suggested by modern scholars.</span> </li> 
        </ol>
       </div>
      </div>

           <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r999302996"><cite id="CITEREFKeilDelitzsch2011" class="citation book cs1">Keil, Carl Friedrich; Delitzsch, Franz (2011). <i>Commentary on the Old Testament</i>.
